The paper analyzes how parties bound by an incomplete contract have an incentive to renegotiate after acquiring new information, examining the issue of parties' investment in the relationship before renegotiation through a simple two-period procurement model.\n\nIn the model, the firm invests in the first period and then learns its production cost, while the sponsor learns its value for the project. The analysis shows that Williamson's underinvestment presumption holds under very general assumptions about bargaining and ex post information asymmetry when the firm's investment is not observable by the sponsor, and that the introduction of a cancellation fee may lead to even less investment.\n\nTirole provided a formal model of underinvestment, illustrating how commitment to a fixed price ex ante may result in inefficient outcomes ex post (i.e., cost overruns).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"a89ac0a6-606a-45db-919d-ba2157095c35","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:03:16.982Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c2edc457-f02b-4377-863a-013cd14949ee","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}

This mechanism is written directly into the code, drastically reducing coordination constraints around voicing dissent and allowing members access to their underlying claims without being bound to futile politics and persuasion.\n\n## Staking and Commitment Mechanisms\n\nRelated to commitment mechanisms, Rite of Moloch is a modular staking app. Prospective DAO members can stake to cohorts. After successfully staking cohort members will receive a soulbound token (SBT). The solution allows for formalising contribution structure through the introduction of a bond. In other words, if an individual wants to become a member they can proclaim this goal, stake a set amount of funds, and work towards their goal. In the case of failure, the funds are slashed and forwarded to the treasury of the DAO.\n\nThe search results show these mechanisms are designed to balance member incentives and commitment within DAO structures, though the specific concept of \"bilateral mutual staking commitment\" wasn't explicitly defined in the available results.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b3ce2e07-e46e-4cfa-8bf5-f3a01254d77c","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:03:18.034Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5862d99f-d50f-41a6-9c4a-e30cfdc46419","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}

Specifically, it analyzes the decisions of a hyperbolic consumer who has access to an imperfect commitment technology: an illiquid asset whose sale must be initiated one period before the sale proceeds are received.\n\n## Main Findings\n\nThe formal model produces several important predictions:\n\n- The model predicts that consumption tracks income and explains why consumers have asset-specific marginal propensities to consume\n- The model implies that financial market innovation may reduce welfare by providing \"too much\" liquidity\n- The model suggests that financial innovation may have caused the ongoing decline in U.S. savings rates, since financial innovation increases liquidity and eliminates implicit commitment opportunities\n\nThis paper has become highly influential in behavioral economics, establishing how imperfect commitment mechanisms through illiquid assets can help individuals overcome the self-control problems created by time-inconsistent preferences.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"3464704d-07b6-40cb-8fb2-dcef710c40a8","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:03:28.837Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d5a18c74-ef6f-48af-89bb-d00152b82563","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}

Here are the key details:\n\n## Publication Details\n\nEric Maskin and Jean Tirole's \"Unforeseen Contingencies and Incomplete Contracts\" was published in the Review of Economic Studies, Volume 66, Issue 1, in 1999, pages 83-114.\n\n## Main Argument\n\nThe paper scrutinizes the conceptual framework commonly used in the incomplete contract literature, which usually assumes that contractual incompleteness is due to the transaction costs of describing—or of even foreseeing—the possible states of nature in advance.\n\nMaskin and Tirole argue that such transaction costs need not interfere with optimal contracting, provided that agents can probabilistically forecast their possible future payoffs (even if other aspects of the state of nature cannot be forecast). In other words, all that is required for optimality is that agents be able to perform dynamic programming.\n\n## Key Findings on Renegotiation\n\nThe optimality result holds very generally provided that parties can commit themselves not to renegotiate. Moreover, the paper points out that renegotiation may be hard to reconcile with a framework that otherwise presumes perfect rationality.\n\nThe paper is available through various academic sources, including the Oxford Academic platform and Harvard Scholar repositories.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"326639cc-e57c-430d-a017-3f39387d5c5c","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:03:30.842Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"f2d6269a-cb1f-4b88-a918-122778776ac1","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}

Here's a summary of what I found:\n\n## Lightning Network Overview\n\nThe Lightning Network (LN) is a payment protocol built on the bitcoin blockchain intended to enable fast transactions among participating nodes and has been proposed as a solution to the bitcoin scalability problem.\n\n## Hashed Timelock Contracts (HTLCs)\n\nHTLCs are the centerpiece of every Lightning Network payment and of any Lightning transaction. A Hash Time Locked Contract or HTLC is a class of payments that use hashlocks and timelocks to require that the receiver of a payment either acknowledge receiving the payment prior to a deadline by generating cryptographic proof of payment or forfeit the ability to claim the payment, returning it to the payer.\n\nMore specifically, a HTLC is an output of an unconfirmed transaction to a separate \"smart contract\" address with properties including: the smart contract can be spent by revealing a \"secret' together with a valid signature of the recipient, and the smart contract can be redeemed after a set amount of time together with a valid signature of the sender.\n\n## Bilateral Payment Channels\n\nA bi-directional payment channel is a payment channel where payments can flow both directions, from Alice to Bob and back to Alice, contrasted with other payment channels where payments can only go one direction. The Lightning Network operates through bidirectional payment channels between two nodes, forming smart contracts that facilitate off-chain transactions.\n\n## Smart Contracts & State Management\n\nParties can update their individual allocations for the ledger entry by creating many transactions spending from the current ledger entry output, with only the most recent version being valid, which is enforced by blockchain-parsable smart-contract scripting.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"467a9625-0d84-440a-aeea-a2b3696e08d8","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:03:33.652Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d8e28238-b825-400a-8466-78c163a0a37d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}

One such derivative instrument is a Contract-for-Difference (CfD), specifically available to renewable generators in some jurisdictions to enable them to hedge against their price risk. CfD is a bilateral arrangement between a generator selling into, and an offtaker buying out of, a centrally cleared pool market for electricity. In this arrangement, the generator subsidizes the offtaker when the spot price is high; whereas, the offtaker subsidizes the generator when the spot price is low. This establishes a synthetic bilateral electricity transaction, operating in parallel to the pool market. Embracing CfD to hedge against price risk presents new risks such as counterparty credit, margining, third-party, and legal risks. They also incur high costs and possess underlying process risks. Decentralized Finance - an overarching term representing financial services built on top of a public blockchain - seems to present particularly compelling opportunities in electricity derivatives for these reasons. Therefore, we propose a novel Decentralized Finance instrument: a blockchain-based marketplace governed by a smart contract to act as a mediator between stakeholders mutually enrolled in bilateral CfD arrangements. The employed smart contract structure autonomously and irrefutably enforces the terms of the CfD, underpinned by a novel collateralization and settlement mechanism. The article studies moral hazard with many agents, focusing on two features novel in a multiagent setting: free riding and competition.\n\n## Key Insights on Incentives and Monitoring\n\nThe free-rider problem implies a new role for the principal: administering incentive schemes that do not balance the budget, which is essential for controlling incentives and suggests that firms in which ownership and labor are partly separated will have an advantage over partnerships in which output is distributed among agents.\n\nA new characterization of informative (hence valuable) monitoring is derived and applied to analyze the value of relative performance evaluation.\n\n## Regarding Mutual Monitoring and Penalties\n\nWhile the search results describe the principal's role and monitoring mechanisms, they don't contain specific details about bilateral monitoring between team members or mutual penalties as discussed in your query. However, Holmström (1982) emphasizes the role of an external monitor or budget breaker to prevent shirking by team members, and subsequent research has explored how peer monitoring (even in weak forms like anonymous monitoring) may resolve the moral hazard in teams problem.\n\nIf you need more detailed information about specific mechanisms of bilateral monitoring and mutual penalties within the paper, you may want to access the full text directly.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"96b90d8e-fed3-42d0-ae4c-103c9097977d","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:03:49.423Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"415fe690-da61-4257-9096-c799885da744","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}

Here are the search results:\n\n## Key Findings:\n\nThe 2003 paper by Szentes and Rosenthal constructs symmetric equilibria for a class of symmetric auction games where two identical bidders bid in three simultaneous first-price sealed-bid auctions for identical objects, with complete information and bidders' marginal valuations increasing for the second object and then decreasing for the third.\n\nIn all cases the support of the mixture that generates the equilibrium is two-dimensional, and it surrounds a three-dimensional set of best responses.\n\nThe structure of this equilibrium is intriguing, in particular because it features a two-dimensional equilibrium support enclosing a three-dimensional best-response set.\n\nThe paper is titled \"Games and Economic Behavior 44 (2003) 114–133\" and was published in the journal Games and Economic Behavior. The full PDF is available at the LSE website. This work is also commonly referred to in the literature as the \"chopstick auction\" due to the identical nature of the objects being auctioned.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"6d80029a-2482-4b73-9b06-651d4967cab6","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:03:49.956Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"8c7bbb5e-26ba-4b1a-9927-bab883a68df8","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}

Beeminder is goal-tracking with teeth that plots your progress on a graph with a Bright Red Line, and if your datapoints cross that line, they take your money.\n\n## Commitment Contracts and Behavioral Economics\n\nBoth platforms leverage commitment contracts, which are grounded in behavioral economic principles. Commitment contracts bring a risk of loss into the present, where the temptations also lie, and augment motivation to succeed. People are generally more motivated to avoid losses than they are to achieve similarly sized gains.\n\n## How stickK Works\n\nTo complete a commitment contract on StickK, a user picks a goal, sets a time limit, chooses stakes that will be lost if the goal isn't completed, and invites friends and family to referee the contract. The purpose is to increase the short-term financial costs of quitting on a long-term goal. Users put down money (say, $200) and state a goal they want to achieve (such as losing ten pounds in a month), and specify what will happen to the money if they don't stick to their commitments.\n\nAssigning a \"referee\" to keep you honest doubles your chance of success, according to an analysis of stickK's users.\n\n## Financial Penalties and Behavioral Mechanisms\n\nStickK relies on two behavioral truths: people do not like losing money (loss aversion), and they are likely to behave better when others are looking. published their seminal paper \"The Costs and Benefits of Ownership: A Theory of Vertical and Lateral Integration\" in 1986 in the Journal of Political Economy.\n\n## Key Contribution\n\nThe paper used incomplete contract theory to answer the question \"What is a firm and what determines its boundaries?\" Their theory of costly contracts emphasizes that contractual rights can be of two types: specific rights and residual rights. When it is costly to list all specific rights over assets in the contract, it may be optimal to let one party purchase all residual rights.\n\n## Theoretical Framework\n\nBoth Grossman and Hart consider the firm to be a collection of assets over which the owners have residual control. They argue that where one party's investment is more important than another's, it is preferable to allocate title to the asset to that party, even if this discourages the other party's investment.\n\n## Significance and Legacy\n\nThe 2016 Nobel Prize in Economics was awarded to Oliver D. Hart and Bengt Holmström for their contribution to contract theory, including incomplete contracts. Here are the key findings:\n\n## Publication Details\n\nRosenthal's 1981 paper was titled \"Games of perfect information, predatory pricing and the chain-store paradox,\" published in the Journal of Economic Theory, volume 25(1), pages 92-100, in August.\n\n## The Centipede Game\n\nThe paper is particularly notable for introducing what became known as the Centipede Game. Rosenthal's Centipede Game was used as an example when criticizing the defense of the backward induction principle. Cooperation in Rosenthal's (1981) centipede game is consistent with common certainty of rationality at the beginning of the game, which challenged the standard backward induction prediction.\n\n## Significance\n\nIn the late 1970s, backward induction came under critical fire when the chain-store paradox, Centipede, and other games questioned its universal virtue, with criticism supported by experimental evidence greatly diminishing the appeal of backward induction in some games.\n\nThe paper has been highly influential in game theory literature, spawning extensive subsequent research on the relationship between backward induction, rationality, and credible commitment in sequential games with perfect information.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"fbd599c8-2b4d-4386-949a-d1777326b9d0","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:04:12.805Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"844f279b-fb57-46a2-865e-d3c8762606df","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}
{"parentUuid":"a0535033-98f8-4a93-888e-9c5eceb77e2a","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"11879b96-a7a4-436f-8823-22ec70947fbf","agentId":"ac54c2225b68cb93d","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01EgWt4m6N4han4MFBBNGh4g","type":"tool_result","content":[{"type":"text","text":"{\"count\": 10, \"papers\": [{\"title\": \"A Survey on Consensus Mechanisms and Mining Strategy Management in Blockchain Networks\", \"authors\": [\"Wenbo Wang\", \"Dinh Thai Hoang\", \"Peizhao Hu\", \"Zehui Xiong\", \"Dusit Niyato\", \"Ping Wang\", \"Yonggang Wen\", \"Dong In Kim\"], \"abstract\": \"The past decade has witnessed the rapid evolution in blockchain technologies, which has attracted tremendous interests from both the research communities and industries. The blockchain network was originated from the Internet financial sector as a decentralized, immutable ledger system for transactional data ordering. Nowadays, it is envisioned as a powerful backbone/framework for decentralized data processing and data-driven self-organization in flat, open-access networks. In particular, the plausible characteristics of decentralization, immutability, and self-organization are primarily owing to the unique decentralized consensus mechanisms introduced by blockchain networks. This survey is motivated by the lack of a comprehensive literature review on the development of decentralized consensus mechanisms in blockchain networks. In this paper, we provide a systematic vision of the organization of blockchain networks. By emphasizing the unique characteristics of decentralized consensus in blockchain networks, our in-depth review of the state-of-the-art consensus protocols is focused on both the perspective of distributed consensus system design and the perspective of incentive mechanism design. From a game-theoretic point of view, we also provide a thorough review of the strategy adopted for self-organization by the individual nodes in the blockchain backbone networks. The paper notes that blockchain technology provides decentralized consensus and potentially enlarges the contracting space through smart contracts. Meanwhile, generating decentralized consensus entails distributing information that necessarily alters the informational environment, and the authors analyze how decentralization relates to consensus quality and how the quintessential features of blockchain remold the landscape of competition.\n\nRegarding game theory and mechanisms, the paper finds that smart contracts can mitigate informational asymmetry and improve welfare and consumer surplus through enhanced entry and competition, yet distributing information during consensus generation may encourage greater collusion. Additionally, the paper highlights one salient economic mechanism through which blockchain facilitates collusion, and explores policy implications, noting that a regulatory solution is to separate usage and consensus generation on blockchains, so that sellers cannot use the consensus-generating information for the purpose of sustaining collusion.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"fde14201-68d1-496d-841d-ae2840f534e2","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:04:25.643Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c9021b35-d792-48a8-af18-d5fc1ab5347a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}

Here's a summary of the key findings:\n\n## Overview of Dominant Assurance Contracts\n\nAn assurance contract, also known as a provision point mechanism, or crowdaction, is a game-theoretic mechanism that facilitates the voluntary creation of public goods and club goods in the face of collective action problems such as the free rider problem.\n\n## Tabarrok's Contribution\n\nDominant assurance contracts, created by Alex Tabarrok, involve an extra component, an entrepreneur who profits when the quorum is reached and pays the signors extra if it is not. An entrepreneur can design a contract where the equilibrium has agents contributing to produce the public good as a dominant strategy.\n\n## How It Works\n\nIf the quorum is not formed, the signors do not pay their share and indeed actively profit from having participated since they keep the money the entrepreneur paid them. Conversely, if the quorum succeeds, the entrepreneur is compensated for taking the risk of the quorum failing. Thus, a player will benefit whether or not the quorum succeeds; if it fails the player reaps a monetary return, and if it succeeds, the player pays only a small amount more than under an assurance contract, and the public good will be provided.\n\n## Game-Theoretic Analysis\n\nThe dominant assurance contract is modeled as a game and the pure strategy equilibrium has agents contributing to the public good as a dominant strategy. Originally proposed by economist Alex Tabarrok, DACs solve the public goods dilemma by removing first-mover risk. DACs make contributing the dominant strategy regardless of whether the project reaches its goal.\n\nThe original paper was published in *Public Choice* in 1998.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"9c8c3784-db4e-4ff0-ba84-b0408e15e791","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:04:42.191Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"71136822-fed0-4227-ade9-bce3c7bb7fcf","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}
{"parentUuid":"5c3c5274-4997-4f03-a434-0aa5b99e9424","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"11879b96-a7a4-436f-8823-22ec70947fbf","agentId":"ac54c2225b68cb93d","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01PnYS3bCAgittdKmZ5kv56B","type":"tool_result","content":"Web search results for query: \"Vitalik Buterin \"griefing\" factor 1:1 ratio deposit slashing penalty Casper proof of stake mechanism 2017\"\n\nLinks: [{\"title\":\"Casper the Friendly Finality Gadget Vitalik Buterin and Virgil Grifﬁth\",\"url\":\"https://arxiv.org/pdf/1710.09437\"},{\"title\":\"Proof of Stake FAQ\",\"url\":\"https://vitalik.ca/general/2017/12/31/pos_faq.html\"},{\"title\":\"The Triangle of Harm\",\"url\":\"https://vitalik.ca/general/2017/07/16/triangle_of_harm.html\"},{\"title\":\"Minimal Slashing Conditions. Last week Yoichi released a blog post… | by Vitalik Buterin | Medium\",\"url\":\"https://medium.com/@VitalikButerin/minimal-slashing-conditions-20f0b500fc6c\"},{\"title\":\"Casper FFG: Consensus Protocol for the Realization of Proof-of-Stake | by Juin Chiu | Unitychain | Medium\",\"url\":\"https://medium.com/unitychain/intro-to-casper-ffg-9ed944d98b2d\"},{\"title\":\"Vitalik Buterin: Proof-of-Stake Is More Secure and Resilient Than Proof-of-Work - Blockonomi\",\"url\":\"https://blockonomi.com/vitalik-buterin-proof-of-stake-is-more-secure-and-resilient-than-proof-of-work/\"},{\"title\":\"(PDF) Casper the Friendly Finality Gadget\",\"url\":\"https://www.researchgate.net/publication/320626951_Casper_the_Friendly_Finality_Gadget\"},{\"title\":\"[1710.09437] Casper the Friendly Finality Gadget\",\"url\":\"https://arxiv.org/abs/1710.09437\"},{\"title\":\"Ethereum Proof-Of-Stake: Vitalik Buterin Shares Casper Contract Code — Steemit\",\"url\":\"https://steemit.com/ethereum/@keysikg/ethereum-proof-of-stake-vitalik-buterin-shares-casper-contract-code\"},{\"title\":\"Vitalik Buterin Tells the Story of His Race With Vlad Zamfir to Implement Proof of Stake Casper | CoinHub.News :: Cryptocurrency news aggregated, curated by you.\",\"url\":\"https://www.coinhub.news/cs/article/trustnodes-vitalik-buterin-tells-the-story-of-his-race-with-vlad-zamfir-to-implement-proof-of-stake-casper\"}]\n\nI've found relevant search results on this topic. Here's what the sources reveal about griefing factors and deposit slashing penalties in Casper:\n\n## Griefing Factor Definition and Analysis\n\nThe griefing factor of a strategy is essentially the amount of money lost by the victims divided by the amount of money lost by the attackers, and the griefing factor of a protocol is the highest griefing factor that it allows. For example, if a protocol allows one to cause another to lose $3 at a cost of $1 to oneself, then the griefing factor is 3.\n\n## Casper's Approach to Griefing Factors\n\nThe main benefits of the second approach (penalizing equivocation) are that (i) it's easier to see that honest validators will not be punished, and (ii) griefing factors are more favorable to honest validators. Casper follows the second flavor, though it is possible that an on-chain mechanism will be added where validators can voluntarily opt-in to signing finality messages of the first flavor, thereby enabling much more efficient light clients.\n\n## Design Philosophy: Upper Bounds on Harm Ratios\n\nThe essence of Casper's philosophy is this: for all four categories of attack, the goal is to put an upper bound on the ratio between the amount of harm suffered by the victims of the attack and the cost to the attacker. In some ways, every design decision in Casper flows out of this principle.\n\nCasper works primarily by punishing equivocation - if one sends two messages that conflict with each other, then one gets very heavily penalized, even if one of those messages aligns with the consensus. Hence, in the event of a finality reversion attack, those who caused the reversion event are penalized, and everyone else is left untouched; the majority can attack the protocol only at heavy cost, and the majority cannot cause the minority to lose money.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"9ebd7db8-af13-4e6c-a160-4c302d1b3ed5","timestamp":"2026-03-19T18:04:43.049Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5c3c5274-4997-4f03-a434-0aa5b99e9424","userType":"external","entrypoint":"sdk-cli","cwd":"/home/claude","sessionId":"83356ac3-79f1-48ae-bdcb-ee1297f7abc4","version":"2.1.79","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"adaptive-wandering-rose"}
{"parentUuid":"9ebd7db8-af13-4e6c-a160-4c302d1b3ed5","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"ac54c2225b68cb93d","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_01QdP2eEsqwTtS7KscYAdU9h","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"I now have comprehensive material across all five research dimensions. Let me compile the full report.\n\n---\n\n# Comprehensive Academic Research Report: Mutual Liquidity Lock (MLL) Protocol -- Theoretical Foundations\n\n## A. COMMITMENT DEVICES IN GAME THEORY AND BEHAVIORAL ECONOMICS\n\n### A.1 Foundational Theory: Schelling\n\nThe foundational work is Thomas C. Schelling's *The Strategy of Conflict* (1960, Harvard University Press). Schelling showed that a party can strengthen its bargaining position by *overtly worsening its own options* -- the paradox of commitment. A general who destroys his retreat route signals to both his enemy and his troops that he is irrevocably committed to battle. Schelling's central insight directly underpins MLL: by depositing funds into a 2-of-2 multisig, both parties voluntarily constrain their own future choices, making their commitment credible.\n\nSchelling also introduced the concept of the **\"threat that leaves something to chance\"** -- the idea that uncertain retaliation is more credible and more efficient than certain retaliation. This is the direct theoretical ancestor of MLL's Russian Roulette mechanism. Schelling's famous analogy: two mountaineers chained together at the edge of a cliff, each manipulating the shared probability of falling. The threat need not be deliberate; it suffices that escalatory actions raise the *probability* of catastrophe.\n\n**Key reference:**\n- Schelling, T.C. (1960). *The Strategy of Conflict*. Harvard University Press. [Learning from Schelling's Strategy of Conflict (Myerson, 2010)](https://home.uchicago.edu/~rmyerson/stratofc2010.pdf)\n\n### A.2 Modern Commitment Device Literature: Bryan, Karlan, Nelson\n\nThe modern survey of commitment devices is **Bryan, Karlan, and Nelson (2010), \"Commitment Devices,\" *Annual Review of Economics*, 2, pp. 671-698.** This paper distinguishes between *hard commitments* (binding constraints that eliminate future choices) and *soft commitments* (which raise the cost of deviating but do not make it impossible). MLL's bleeding penalty (0.5%/day drain) is a soft commitment -- it raises the cost of defection continuously. The fund-freeze mechanism of Russian Roulette is a hard commitment -- funds become permanently inaccessible.\n\nBryan et al. also identify a critical design question: the *demand* for commitment devices. People with self-control problems (modeled via hyperbolic discounting) may voluntarily seek commitment. The bilateral nature of MLL is distinctive: both parties simultaneously demand commitment from each other and supply it to each other.\n\n**Key references:**\n- Bryan, G., Karlan, D., and Nelson, S. (2010). [Commitment Devices](https://www.annualreviews.org/doi/pdf/10.1146/annurev.economics.102308.124324). *Annual Review of Economics*, 2, 671-698.\n- Laibson, D. (1997). [Golden Eggs and Hyperbolic Discounting](https://scholar.harvard.edu/files/laibson/files/golden_eggs_and_hyperbolic_discounting.pdf). *Quarterly Journal of Economics*, 112(2), 443-477. -- Formal model of how illiquid (committed) assets can serve as imperfect commitment devices against present-biased preferences.\n\n### A.3 Preference for Commitment: Gul and Pesendorfer\n\n**Gul, F. and Pesendorfer, W. (2001). [\"Temptation and Self-Control,\"](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1111/1468-0262.00252) *Econometrica*, 69(6), 1403-1435.** This paper axiomatizes the *preference for commitment*: an agent strictly prefers a smaller menu (fewer options) to a larger one, because the larger set contains tempting alternatives. In MLL terms, the 2-of-2 multisig removes the tempting option of unilateral withdrawal. The cost of self-control is measured by the gap between the most tempting choice and the actual choice. The 2025 paper by **Liang and Grant, [\"Ulysses Revisited\"](https://doi.org/10.1007/s11238-025-10037-z), *Theory and Decision* (2025)** extends this to cases where self-control is genuinely binding (the Ulysses case), showing that existing models (Gul-Pesendorfer) cannot accommodate situations where an agent *must* have external control -- a case more aligned with MLL's mandatory structure.\n\n### A.4 Williamson: Hostage Exchange and Credible Commitments\n\n**Williamson, O.E. (1983). [\"Credible Commitments: Using Hostages to Support Exchange,\"](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/4723548_Credible_Commitments_Using_Hostages_to_Support_Exchange) *American Economic Review*, 73(4), 519-540.** Williamson's framework is directly analogous to MLL. In transaction cost economics, when parties make relationship-specific investments, they face a hold-up problem. Williamson proposes that parties can post \"hostages\" -- valuable assets that would be forfeited in the event of opportunistic behavior -- to make their commitments credible. MLL's mutual deposits are precisely Williamson's \"hostages\": each party's locked funds serve as a credible pledge to the other.\n\nWilliamson distinguishes credible *commitments* (used to support exchange) from credible *threats* (used in rivalry). MLL uses both: the deposits are commitments, while the Russian Roulette mechanism is a threat.\n\n### A.5 Practical Commitment Platforms\n\nPractical implementations of commitment devices with financial penalties exist in the non-crypto world:\n- **[stickK](https://www.stickk.com/)** (founded by Dean Karlan and colleagues at Yale) allows users to set goals and stake money, which goes to a designated recipient (including \"anti-charities\") upon failure. This leverages loss aversion.\n- **[Beeminder](https://blog.beeminder.com/anticharity/)** charges users money when they deviate from their self-set goals.\n\nThese are *unilateral* commitment devices (one person commits to themselves). MLL is notably *bilateral* -- both parties are simultaneously committers and enforcers. This bilateral structure has limited precedent in the commitment device literature, which is a gap MLL could claim to fill.\n\n### A.6 State of the Art Assessment\n\nThe literature on commitment devices is well-developed for *unilateral* commitments (individual self-control). Bilateral/mutual commitment devices with financial stakes have received far less formal treatment. Williamson's hostage exchange is the closest conceptual analogue, but it was not formalized as a game-theoretic mechanism. The intersection of bilateral commitment, automated enforcement, and graduated penalties appears to be genuinely novel territory.\n\n---\n\n## B. MECHANISM DESIGN FOR BILATERAL AGREEMENTS WITHOUT TRUSTED THIRD PARTIES\n\n### B.1 The Myerson-Satterthwaite Impossibility\n\nThe starting point for any bilateral mechanism design analysis is **Myerson, R.B. and Satterthwaite, M.A. (1983). [\"Efficient Mechanisms for Bilateral Trading,\"](https://www.cs.princeton.edu/courses/archive/spr10/cos444/papers/myerson_satterthwaite83.pdf) *Journal of Economic Theory*, 29, 265-281.** The theorem proves that no mechanism can simultaneously achieve: (i) individual rationality, (ii) budget balance, (iii) incentive compatibility, and (iv) ex-post efficiency in bilateral trade with private valuations.\n\n**Relevance to MLL:** MLL sidesteps the Myerson-Satterthwaite problem because it is not a bilateral *trade* mechanism -- both parties are depositing into a shared pool rather than exchanging goods for money. However, the impossibility result is a cautionary benchmark: any claim that MLL achieves first-best outcomes needs careful qualification about what is being optimized.\n\n### B.2 Incomplete Contracts: Hart, Moore, Tirole\n\n**Hart, O. and Moore, J. (1988). [\"Incomplete Contracts and Renegotiation,\"](https://www.econometricsociety.org/publications/econometrica/1988/07/01/incomplete-contracts-and-renegotiation) *Econometrica*, 56(4), 755-785.** This paper shows that when contracts cannot specify all contingencies, parties must build in revision mechanisms. A key result: the possibility of renegotiation *severely constrains* what revision mechanisms can achieve. This is profoundly relevant to MLL: the smart contract is, by design, a *complete* contract (all contingencies are coded), which avoids the Hart-Moore renegotiation problem -- but at the cost of rigidity.\n\n**Grossman, S.J. and Hart, O.D. (1986). [\"The Costs and Benefits of Ownership: A Theory of Vertical and Lateral Integration,\"](https://ideas.repec.org/a/ucp/jpolec/v94y1986i4p691-719.html) *Journal of Political Economy*, 94(4), 691-719.** The property rights theory of the firm: when contracts are incomplete, residual control rights matter. In MLL, there are essentially *no* residual control rights -- neither party can act unilaterally except to trigger Russian Roulette. This is a radical departure from the Hart-Grossman framework.\n\n**Maskin, E. and Tirole, J. (1999). [\"Unforeseen Contingencies and Incomplete Contracts,\"](https://scholar.harvard.edu/files/maskin/files/unforeseen_contingencies_and_incomplete_contracts.pdf) *Review of Economic Studies*, 66(1), 83-114.** Maskin and Tirole argue that even with unforeseen contingencies, optimal contracting is possible if agents can forecast their future *payoffs* (even if they cannot describe future states). But this optimality depends on the ability to commit not to renegotiate. MLL's smart contract enforces precisely this: no renegotiation is possible (the code is immutable), which, per Maskin-Tirole, should *enable* optimal outcomes.\n\n**Tirole, J. (1986). [\"Procurement and Renegotiation,\"](https://www.journals.uchicago.edu/doi/abs/10.1086/261372) *Journal of Political Economy*, 94(2), 235-259.** Shows that cancellation fees (analogous to MLL's bleeding penalty) may paradoxically *reduce* investment if they alter the renegotiation dynamics. A formal analysis of MLL should consider whether the bleeding penalty changes investment incentives at the margin.\n\n### B.3 Automated Penalty Mechanisms in Smart Contracts\n\n**Asgaonkar, A. and Krishnamachari, B. (2018/2019). [\"Solving the Buyer and Seller's Dilemma: A Dual-Deposit Escrow Smart Contract for Provably Cheat-Proof Delivery and Payment for a Digital Good without a Trusted Mediator,\"](https://arxiv.org/abs/1806.08379) IEEE International Conference on Blockchain and Cryptocurrency (ICBC), 2019.** This is the closest published mechanism to MLL's deposit structure. Both buyer and seller place deposits exceeding the value of the good. The paper proves that the **Subgame Perfect Nash Equilibrium** for this extensive-form game is for both parties to cooperate honestly, provided deposits exceed the good's value. MLL generalizes this to an ongoing relationship rather than a one-shot trade.\n\n**Cong, L.W. and He, Z. (2019). [\"Blockchain Disruption and Smart Contracts,\"](https://academic.oup.com/rfs/article-abstract/32/5/1754/5427778) *Review of Financial Studies*, 32(5), 1754-1797.** Formal economic analysis of how smart contracts enlarge the contracting space, potentially mitigating informational asymmetry but also potentially facilitating collusion through information distribution. Relevant to MLL because it establishes that the smart-contract format genuinely changes what can be committed to.\n\n### B.4 Holmstrom: Budget-Breaking and Teams\n\n**Holmstrom, B. (1982). [\"Moral Hazard in Teams,\"](https://people.duke.edu/~qc2/BA532/1982%20Rand%20Holmstrom%20team.pdf) *Bell Journal of Economics*, 13, 324-340.** Shows that in teams, a \"budget-breaker\" (an external party who absorbs surplus or deficit) can resolve free-riding. In MLL, the smart contract itself acts as the budget-breaker: the bleeding penalty transfers value from the defector to the compliant party, and the Russian Roulette permanently destroys value (the ultimate budget-breaking).\n\n### B.5 Burning Money as Commitment\n\nThe concept of **money burning** as commitment is formalized in **Ben-Porath, E. and Dekel, E. (1992). [\"Signaling Future Actions and the Potential for Sacrifice,\"](https://cet.econ.northwestern.edu/dekel/pdf/signaling-future-actions.pdf) *Journal of Economic Theory*, 57, 36-51.** Deliberately destroying value can serve as a credible signal of commitment. The Russian Roulette mechanism in MLL is a sophisticated version of money burning: the *threat* of destruction provides commitment, and the probabilistic nature keeps the cost of the threat lower than certain destruction while still being credible.\n\n---\n\n## C. BRINKMANSHIP AND GRADUATED ESCALATION\n\n### C.1 Schelling's Brinkmanship\n\nSchelling's original formulation of brinkmanship (in *The Strategy of Conflict* and *Arms and Influence*, 1966) describes it as a \"competition in risk-taking\" -- a situation where both parties gradually escalate, increasing the probability of mutual disaster, until one side backs down. The key innovation is that **the threat is not to do something terrible, but to increase the probability that something terrible will happen**.\n\nMLL's Russian Roulette mechanism is a direct implementation of this logic. Each invocation increases the probability of permanent fund freezing (1/6, 1/5, 1/4, 1/3, 1/2, 1/1 -- certainty by the sixth pull). The 10-day cooldown between invocations creates the \"graduated\" character of brinkmanship, giving the other party time to respond or concede.\n\n### C.2 Powell's Formal Models\n\n**Powell, R. (1988). [\"Nuclear Brinkmanship with Two-Sided Incomplete Information,\"](https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/american-political-science-review/article/abs/nuclear-brinkmanship-with-twosided-incomplete-information/58778528F92C8209E2AC368EE4362734) *American Political Science Review*, 82(1), 155-178.** Powell models brinkmanship as a sequential bargaining game with two-sided incomplete information about each party's \"resolve.\" Key findings with direct MLL relevance:\n- The party with the greatest resolve may *not* prevail -- a state may be *less* likely to prevail the greater its resolve.\n- Increasing stakes in the status quo may not deter challenges.\n- Reducing misperception may *destabilize* a crisis.\n\n**Powell, R. (1990). *Nuclear Deterrence Theory: The Search for Credibility*. Cambridge University Press.** The book-length treatment formalizes brinkmanship as escalation along a risk continuum. Powell distinguishes between \"the strategy that leaves something to chance\" (Schelling's concept) and \"the strategy of limited retaliation.\" MLL's Russian Roulette is closer to the former: each invocation does not directly harm the opponent but raises the shared probability of mutual destruction.\n\n### C.3 Herrera, Mace, Nunez: Political Brinkmanship\n\n**Herrera, H., Mace, A., and Nunez, M. (2025). [\"Political Brinkmanship and Compromise,\"](https://doi.org/10.1111/iere.12760) *International Economic Review*.** This very recent paper studies how do-or-die threats affect bargaining. Key finding: **brinkmanship threats improve the probability of agreement but also increase the risk of crisis.** Critically, *brinkmanship reduces welfare when gridlock is most severe* -- precisely the cases where one might think it is most needed. For MLL, this suggests the Russian Roulette mechanism may be most effective for moderate disagreements but could backfire in severe disputes.\n\n### C.4 Pauly and McDermott: Psychology of Brinkmanship\n\n**Pauly, R.B.C. and McDermott, R. (2023). [\"The Psychology of Nuclear Brinkmanship,\"](https://direct.mit.edu/isec/article/47/3/9/114669/The-Psychology-of-Nuclear-Brinkmanship) *International Security*, 47(3), 9-49.** Argues that Schelling's model underestimates the role of emotion. Three mechanisms of brinkmanship -- accidents, self-control, and control of others -- can introduce chance into bargaining even when leaders retain choice. For MLL, the analog is that *even knowing the probabilities*, a party may trigger Russian Roulette out of anger or spite, not rational calculation. This undermines pure game-theoretic predictions.\n\n### C.5 Comparison: MLL's Russian Roulette vs. Classical Models\n\n| Feature | Schelling (1960) | Powell (1988/1990) | MLL Russian Roulette |\n|---------|-----------------|-------------------|---------------------|\n| Probability source | Exogenous (\"nature\") | Endogenous (resolve) | Coded (1/N counter) |\n| Escalation | Continuous | Sequential | Discrete, 6 steps |\n| Information | Incomplete | Two-sided incomplete | Complete (code is public) |\n| Destruction | Physical (nuclear) | Physical | Financial (fund freeze) |\n| Reversibility | Irreversible | Irreversible | Irreversible (frozen) |\n| Cooldown | No formal structure | Model-dependent | 10-day cooldown |\n\nA critical difference: in classical brinkmanship, the probability of disaster is often *exogenous* or tied to unobservable \"resolve.\" In MLL, the probability is deterministic and publicly known (the counter N is set at contract creation, and the check count is on-chain). This transforms it from a game of incomplete information to one of complete information with a stochastic element -- a meaningful formal distinction.\n\n---\n\n## D. PRIOR ART IN CRYPTO/DEFI\n\n### D.1 Dual-Deposit Escrow Contracts\n\nThe closest DeFi prior art is the **dual-deposit escrow** pattern, as formalized in Asgaonkar and Krishnamachari (2018, arXiv:1806.08379). Both parties deposit collateral exceeding the transaction value. If either party cheats, they lose their deposit. The SPNE is honest cooperation. A GitHub implementation exists: [Gas-Optimized Mutually Assured Destruction Escrow Smart Contract](https://github.com/Aarushi5g/Gas-Optimized-Mutually-Assured-Destruction-Escrow-Smart-Contract). However, these are designed for *one-shot* transactions, not ongoing relationships. MLL's continuous deposit requirement and time-varying penalties are novel extensions.\n\n### D.2 Moloch DAO and Ragequit\n\n**[Moloch DAO](https://molochdao.com/)** introduced the \"ragequit\" mechanism: any member can exit at any time by burning their shares for a pro-rata claim on the treasury. This is the *inverse* of MLL -- Moloch enables easy exit; MLL penalizes it. The related **[Rite of Moloch](https://github.com/rite-of-moloch/RiteOfMoloch-v1.0)** protocol requires prospective DAO members to stake funds, which are slashed upon failure to meet commitments. This is closer to MLL's unilateral staking penalty, though it is not bilateral.\n\n### D.3 Lightning Network / State Channels\n\nThe **[Bitcoin Lightning Network](https://lightning.network/)** uses bilateral payment channels with **Hashed Timelock Contracts (HTLCs)**. Two parties lock funds in a 2-of-2 multisig and update their balance off-chain. Either party can close the channel by publishing the latest state on-chain. Penalty mechanisms exist: if one party tries to publish an old (favorable to them) state, the other party can claim *all* funds in the channel. This is structurally similar to MLL's bleeding penalty but operates on a different timescale (immediate vs. gradual).\n\n### D.4 Ethereum Casper and Slashing\n\nEthereum's **[Casper FFG](https://arxiv.org/abs/1710.09437)** (Buterin and Griffith, 2017) introduced the concept of **slashing conditions** and the **griefing factor** -- the ratio of harm suffered by victims to cost borne by the attacker. Casper aims to keep griefing factors bounded (ideally 1:1 -- an attacker must lose at least as much as they cause others to lose). MLL's bleeding penalty has a griefing factor of 0: the defector loses funds that flow to the compliant party, so the \"attacker\" (defector) loses and the \"victim\" gains. The Russian Roulette mechanism has an *infinite* griefing factor -- the triggering party loses the same amount as the other party (funds are destroyed, not transferred).\n\n### D.5 Kleros and Decentralized Arbitration\n\n**[Kleros](https://kleros.io/)** is a decentralized dispute resolution protocol where jurors stake tokens and vote on disputes. It solves the problem of bilateral disagreements by introducing a crowdsourced third party. MLL explicitly *avoids* third-party arbitration -- its endgame is either mutual agreement or permanent destruction. This is a fundamental design choice: Kleros optimizes for dispute *resolution*; MLL optimizes for dispute *prevention*.\n\n### D.6 Dominant Assurance Contracts\n\n**Tabarrok, A. (1998). [\"The Private Provision of Public Goods via Dominant Assurance Contracts,\"](https://mason.gmu.edu/~atabarro/PrivateProvision.pdf) *Public Choice*, 96(3-4), 345-362.** Dominant assurance contracts make contribution the dominant strategy by paying contributors if the funding goal is not met. While designed for public goods rather than bilateral commitments, the mechanism shares a key insight with MLL: properly structured financial incentives can make cooperation the dominant strategy.\n\n### D.7 Assessment of Novelty\n\nNo existing DeFi protocol combines all of MLL's features:\n1. Bilateral (not unilateral) staking\n2. Continuous (not one-shot) deposits\n3. Graduated bleeding penalty for defection\n4. Probabilistic destruction mechanism (Russian Roulette)\n5. Permanent fund freeze as terminal outcome\n\nThe dual-deposit escrow is the closest, but it lacks features 2-5. Moloch DAO has exit penalties but not bilateral lock-in. Lightning Network has bilateral channels but with immediate (not graduated) penalties and no destruction mechanism.\n\n---\n\n## E. FORMAL PROPERTIES: WHAT A FORMAL ANALYSIS MUST PROVE\n\n### E.1 Incentive Compatibility (IC)\n\nA formal analysis must show that within MLL, honest behavior (continuing deposits, not triggering Russian Roulette without cause) is incentive-compatible -- i.e., each party's best response to the other's honest play is also honest play. The dual-deposit escrow literature (Asgaonkar and Krishnamachari, 2018) demonstrates how to prove this for one-shot games. For MLL, the analysis must be extended to a *repeated* game with ongoing deposits.\n\n**Key framework:** Abreu, D., Pearce, D., and Stacchetti, E. (1990). [\"Toward a Theory of Discounted Repeated Games with Imperfect Monitoring,\"](https://www.econometricsociety.org/publications/econometrica/1990/09/01/toward-theory-discounted-repeated-games-imperfect-monitoring) *Econometrica*, 58(5), 1041-1063. Their \"self-generation\" technique allows properties of supergame equilibria to be deduced from static decomposition into flow payoffs and continuation values. MLL's periodic deposits create a natural period structure for this analysis.\n\n### E.2 Individual Rationality (IR)\n\nBoth parties must be (weakly) better off entering MLL than not entering it. This requires:\n- The expected value of the locked funds (including DeFi yield) minus the probability-weighted cost of the Russian Roulette risk exceeds the outside option.\n- Both parties must *ex ante* prefer the constrained MLL to the unconstrained alternative.\n\nThe Myerson-Satterthwaite theorem (1983) provides the key impossibility benchmark: even with optimally designed mechanisms, efficiency may require violating individual rationality or budget balance. MLL's design must be checked against this.\n\n### E.3 Subgame Perfect Equilibrium (SPE) Existence\n\nThe critical question is whether \"cooperate and continue depositing\" constitutes a Subgame Perfect Equilibrium. The analysis must consider:\n- **Endgame reasoning:** In a finite-horizon version of MLL, backward induction would unravel cooperation (the centipede game problem, per Rosenthal, 1981). MLL avoids this because the horizon is potentially *infinite* (deposits continue indefinitely), which allows folk-theorem-style results to sustain cooperation.\n- **Deviation payoffs:** What happens if one party stops depositing? The bleeding penalty (0.5%/day) must be calibrated so that the deviant's payoff from stopping is strictly less than the payoff from continuing.\n- **Russian Roulette subgame:** When one party triggers the mechanism, the other faces a choice: concede (agree to a peaceful exit) or wait (accept the probability of fund freeze). The SPNE of this subgame depends on the relative values of peaceful exit vs. expected value of continuing.\n\n### E.4 Mechanism Design Desiderata\n\nDrawing on the standard mechanism design framework (as surveyed in [Jackson, M.O., \"Mechanism Theory\"](https://web.stanford.edu/~jacksonm/mechtheo.pdf), Stanford working paper), a formal analysis of MLL should address:\n\n| Property | Requirement | MLL Status |\n|----------|-------------|------------|\n| Incentive Compatibility | Honest play is best response | Needs proof for repeated game |\n| Individual Rationality | Participation is voluntary and beneficial | Depends on outside option and yield |\n| Budget Balance | No external subsidy needed | Yes (closed system, except yield) |\n| Ex-post Efficiency | No waste of resources | Violated by Russian Roulette (fund destruction) |\n| Collusion-proofness | Parties cannot game the mechanism together | N/A (only 2 parties) |\n| Renegotiation-proofness | Equilibrium survives if parties could renegotiate | Critical question (see below) |\n\n### E.5 Renegotiation-Proofness\n\nThis is perhaps the most subtle formal property. If both parties prefer to deviate *together* from the prescribed equilibrium (e.g., by both stopping deposits and splitting the pool), the equilibrium is not renegotiation-proof. Since MLL allows peaceful 2-of-2 exit, renegotiation is built into the mechanism. The question becomes: **does the threat of Russian Roulette survive renegotiation?** If both parties know the other would prefer peaceful exit to Russian Roulette, then the threat is never credible. This connects to Hart and Moore's (1988) finding that renegotiation constrains the feasible mechanism space.\n\nA key formal challenge: proving that the *asymmetry of preferred outcomes* (each party may prefer a different split) prevents renegotiation from undermining the commitment structure.\n\n### E.6 Specific Formal Results Needed\n\n1. **Theorem (Cooperation Equilibrium):** There exists a discount factor threshold delta* such that for all delta > delta*, the strategy profile \"both parties deposit each period; neither triggers Russian Roulette\" is a Subgame Perfect Equilibrium.\n\n2. **Theorem (Deterrence):** The bleeding penalty rate r and the Russian Roulette probability sequence {1/6, 1/5, ..., 1/1} are sufficient to deter unilateral defection, given specific conditions on the deposit/pool ratio.\n\n3. **Theorem (Individual Rationality):** Under specific DeFi yield assumptions, the ex ante expected utility of entering MLL exceeds the outside option for both parties.\n\n4. **Proposition (Inefficiency of Russian Roulette):** The Russian Roulette mechanism is ex-post inefficient (it destroys value) but may be ex-ante efficient (the threat of destruction sustains cooperation that would otherwise be impossible). This parallels the classic result that mutually assured destruction is ex-post irrational but ex-ante rational.\n\n5. **Proposition (Renegotiation):** The MLL equilibrium is renegotiation-proof if and only if the parties' preferred splits are sufficiently divergent.\n\n---\n\n## SUMMARY OF KEY REFERENCES\n\n### Commitment Devices\n- Schelling, T.C. [\"The Triangle of Harm.\"](https://vitalik.ca/general/2017/07/16/triangle_of_harm.html) Blog post on griefing factors.\n\n### Repeated Games\n- Abreu, D., Pearce, D., Stacchetti, E. (1990). \"Toward a Theory of Discounted Repeated Games with Imperfect Monitoring.\" *Econometrica*, 58(5), 1041-1063.\n\n---\n\n## OVERALL ASSESSMENT\n\nMLL occupies a genuinely novel position at the intersection of several well-established literatures. Its closest theoretical antecedents are:\n1. **Williamson's hostage exchange** (bilateral deposits as commitment)\n2. **Schelling's brinkmanship** (Russian Roulette as probabilistic threat)\n3. **Dual-deposit escrow contracts** (mutual financial stakes in smart contracts)\n4. **Repeated game theory** (ongoing deposits sustaining cooperation)\n\nThe main theoretical gaps that MLL addresses and that would need formal treatment:\n- No existing model combines *bilateral* commitment devices with *graduated probabilistic destruction* in a *repeated* game setting.\n- The interaction between the bleeding penalty (continuous soft commitment) and Russian Roulette (discrete hard commitment) has no direct precedent in the literature.\n- The \"frozen trust\" endgame (funds locked but yield continues) is an unusual terminal condition that modifies standard brinkmanship outcomes -- the \"disaster\" is partial rather than total.\n\nA rigorous formal paper on MLL would need to build on the Abreu-Pearce-Stacchetti repeated game framework, incorporate the brinkmanship structure from Powell, and prove SPE existence using techniques from mechanism design. The key novel contribution would be showing how the combination of bleeding penalties and probabilistic destruction creates a mechanism that is incentive-compatible, individually rational, and (conditionally) renegotiation-proof.\n\nSources:\n- [Learning from Schelling's Strategy of Conflict](https://home.uchicago.edu/~rmyerson/stratofc2010.pdf)\n- [Commitment Devices -
